2. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 43 by Arlene Mccarthy (H−0271/97)

Subject: Use of structural funds in the UK

Following the Commissioner's statement in the Financial Times on 29 January, in which she said that 'in a number of UK regions they are asking the EU to fill in the gaps which are due to a lack of public services in certain areas' and went on to ask 'is the organization of childcare services, the replacement of reduced public services and utilities, a European or a national task in a rich country like the UK?', could the Commissioner comment on whether handing £1/2 billion in taxpayers' money to privatized utilities is a good use of funds? Would this money not have been better used to support job creation and training places for women, while helping to support childcare costs? If it is not legally possible for these utilities to pay back EU grants after privatization, would the Commissioner agree that it is a poor use of scarce resources representing poor value for money?

Answer

The remarks to which you refer, Mrs McCarthy, are not in contradiction to the Commission"s determination to promote – to an increasing extent – equality of opportunity, with the aid of the structural funds. The provision of childcare facilities can be crucial to the potential success of measures to assist education and training, as well as access to the employment market, for women and single parents. There are a number of excellent examples of such facilities, which have been assisted within the framework of structural policy objectives or regional development strategies.

However, I should like to make it clear – and that was the intention of my remarks, the remarks to which you, Mrs McCarthy, are alluding – that it is primarily the responsibility of the Member States to provide the basic public infrastructure, including social services and childcare facilities. Unfortunately, this does not seem to be the case everywhere.

Making childcare facilities available via the structural funds can only be done as part of a regional development strategy or in order to facilitate access to the employment market for disadvantaged groups of people, in this case mothers.

Let me also make it clear that in no way does the Commission provide financial support for privatization, which one might, mistakenly, deduce from your question.

As I told the Committee on Regional Policy on 20 March, on the subject of privatization, since 1989 the privatized utilities, like the private sector in general, can apply for co‐financing for their projects, from the European Regional Development Fund.

At a time when public resources are in short supply, private co‐financing can help to mobilize more financial resources for regional development. As far as Community assistance is concerned, the deciding factor is that the original purpose of the aid should continue to be guaranteed after privatization.

***

Question No 44 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0274/97)

Subject: Revival of declining industrial regions in Greece

Measure 1.4 of sub‐programme 1 on infrastructures in the operational programme for industry in respect of Greece (1994‐1999) is concerned with the revival of declining industrial regions and has been endowed with a budget of ECU 105 m. Bearing in mind that, if they receive appropriate support, industrial undertakings operating in disadvantaged regions can make an important contribution not only to the industrial revival of the regions in question, but also to the development and improvement of the economy generally, will the Commission say what has been the take‐up rate so far for the specific measure referred to above?

Answer

In Measure 1.4, ’Revival of declining industrial regions’, of the Operational Programme (OP) for industry, within the framework of the Community Aid Programme for 1994‐1999 in respect of Greece, with the exception of the expenditure for studies concerning the integrated development of the regions concerned, up to 31 December 1996 no money had been paid out.

With the agreement of the Commission, the Greek Government recently published a call for expressions of interest in respect of these measures, aimed at SMEs in the said regions.

The SMEs can submit their applications up to 31 May. After that date, the process of selecting projects will begin. The awarding of the grants is planned for July 1997. The task of administering the grants will be entrusted to seven intermediate regional authorities. The projects should start being implemented during the second half of 1997. The plan is to provide assistance for business plans for companies with between 3 and 250 employees and a turnover of between DR 70 million (ECU 230, 000) and DR 1 billion (ECU 3.3 million). Eligible measures include the following: productive investments, organization of production and distribution, acquisition of patents and technologies, certification under ISO standards, acquisition of specialized managers.

Large undertakings in declining industrial regions are already able to apply for grants under the assistance regulation (Law 1892), which is also co‐financed by the Operational Programme for industry in respect of Greece (1994‐1999).

Measure 1.4 also makes provision for assisting industrial infrastructures, for which proposals are expected from local authorities, and for the creation of auxiliary structures (offices to accompany industrial change) which are intended to give support to the contractors and coordinate the measures at local level.

Question No 45 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0276/97)

Subject: Major delays by the Commission in making payments under PACTE and RECITE projects

Will the Commission state how much funding Danish municipal and regional authorities are entitled to under the PACTE and RECITE programmes?

When does it intend to make the payments to which municipal and regional authorities are entitled?

Will it alter the internal procedures between DG 16, which is responsible for the projects, and the payment and monitoring departments, DGs 19 and 20, so as to ensure that they work properly?

What steps will it take to ensure that cooperation with the decentralized structures in the form of technical offices in the Member States functions as intended and in such a way that major delays do not become the general rule?

Does it agree that the major delays in making payments under various programmes raise doubts about the Commission's credibility and make it hard for project participants to retain confidence in the Commission?

Answer

The Danish municipal and regional authorities are involved in three projects under the RECITE project, for which they receive more than ECU 1.8 million. Of that amount, only ECU 323, 433 is still outstanding, for the two projects that are still running at present. The payments for ENVIRONET will be completed in the next few weeks, once accounting problems (ECU/national currency) have been cleared up. As for the ROC‐NORD project, the Commission is awaiting the final report. The local partners will be charged for this delay.

As part of the PACTE programme, the municipal and regional authorities are involved in 22 projects, involving Community co‐financing amounting to approximately ECU 1.5 million. The payments for projects in 1990 to 1992 have been settled. For 1993 and 1994, the payment procedure is in operation. For projects where the contracts were signed in 1995, the first advance payment has been paid to the administrative office.

As far as RECITE is concerned, there were some delays as a result of the time limits for the tendering procedure for the renewal of contracts with the technical assistance office.

The administration of the interregional cooperation projects, Mrs Hansen, has regrettably proved to be difficult, on both sides, that of the recipients as well as that of the Commission. the delays have be caused in part by the complexity of the administration, and in part by the lack of experience which was a characteristic of the first generation of interregional cooperation projects.

The Commission has therefore, on the basis of experience in partnership gained so far, developed new guidelines for project administration, which in future should ensure the more rapid and more efficient administration of interregional cooperation projects:

‐ the publication of administrative guidelines for project leaders, in which the method of procedure is laid down;

‐ an explanation of the rules that have to be observed, both at the preliminary stage (for example, when organizing the DIRECTORIA V, and also at a later stage (further training workshops for project leaders);

‐ accompanying measures and technical assistance for the cooperating partners, which are to be provided on a larger scale and more systematically than in the past.

Question No 49 by Sören Wibe (H−0215/97)

Subject: Impact on the CAP of the growing numbers of vegetarians

On 11 March 1997, the Guardian newspaper reported a claim by the Vegetarian Society that 38 % of 11‐year‐olds in Britain were avoiding eating beef, and one in five was avoiding eating most meat following the BSE scare; most people became vegetarian in their teens, and these figures indicated an increase in the numbers of vegetarians in Britain in a few years' time. In Sweden, too, there are considerably more vegetarians amongst the young generation than amongst the older generation. This is perhaps a continuing trend of which account should be taken by the CAP.

It could mean a steady fall in meat consumption and an increase in the consumption of a variety of fruit and vegetables, which could lead to overproduction of meat in the EU.

Is the Commission studying the public's changing eating habits and examining whether demand for the production of meat and vegetables will change as a result? Or has it concluded that this is merely an insignificant consumer fad?

Answer

The Commission follows trends in the consumption of food products in the Community very closely and is aware of present changes in eating habits.

In regard to the particular case of meat consumption following the BSE crisis, estimates available to the Commission show that the drop in beef consumption in 1996 was more than offset by increased consumption of other kinds of meat (especially pork and chicken). This would suggest that the increase in the number of vegetarians has to date had no more than a limited impact on overall meat consumption in the Community.

***

Question No 50 by Maj‐Lis Lööw (H−0216/97)

Subject: Production of alcohol‐free and low‐alcohol wines

There are several reasons why alcohol‐free beverages should be promoted as alternative drinks for social settings, as evidenced by Member States' increasingly stringent drink‐drive laws.

Alcohol‐free beers are now available in most countries, but alcohol‐free wine is still a rarity, despite the fact that much progress has been made with product development in that area.

Why could the EU not support the production of alcohol‐free and low‐alcohol wines from its surpluses and also promote the marketing of such wines?

Answer

With Community legislation as it is at present, the Community cannot agree to the Honourable Member's request since alcohol‐free wine or wine with a low alcohol content is not defined at Community level.

On the other hand, what is defined very precisely at Community level is wine and Community legislation reserves the name ‚wine’ for products which meet that definition, which calls for among other things, a minimum alcoholic content.

However, to take into account practices in certain countries, Community legislation currently permits the Member States, without prejudice to any harmonizing provisions which may be introduced in future, to allow the word ‚wine’ to be used in composite names in such a way that any confusion with wine is impossible. The European Court of Justice has rules that ‚alcohol‐free wine’ could be such a composite name.

Moreover, since 1986 the Community has been implementing a programme to promote grape juice, which is a natural product more widely consumed than alcohol‐free wine. The results so far have been satisfactory.

Question No 51 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0238/97)

Subject: Common organization of the market in bananas

In view of the conclusions reached by the World Trade Organization panel as regards the common organization of the market in bananas, what action is the Council proposing to take in order to protect Community producers?

Question No 59 by Tommy Waidelich (H−0272/97)

Subject: EU trade policy on bananas

According to a decision by the World Trade Organization, the EU's rules on bananas are in conflict with current free trade agreements. Does the Commission propose complying with the WTO's decision?

Joint answer

The Community"s rules on bananas are currently being investigated by a World Trade Organization panel as part of the WTO"s procedure for resolving disputes. The panel has recently submitted a draft report on these rules, which is being treated as confidential, to the parties involved in the dispute, who will then be able to comment on it.

The Commission has already examined the factual content of this report, in order to check that no false conclusions have been drawn, and it has pointed out to the authors a number of substantive errors.

Afterwards, following a hearing, the Commission will examine the actual legal arguments. If these arguments prove to be wrong, then under the WTO"s procedure for resolving disputes the case may go to appeal. The Commission will check to see whether such a step would be advisable.

However, it should be emphasized that only on the basis of a final decision by the WTO can we decide how the Commission can continue to support producers in the EC and in the ACP countries, and therefore any conclusions regarding the Community"s reactions would be premature.

Question No 52 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0240/97)

Subject: COM in tobacco

What effect will the reform of the COM in the new tobacco sector have on employment? Will it have negative economic and social consequences? Is there not a danger that the outcome will be not quality, but a range of adverse effects? Will the result not be to make price the criterion of quality? Does the Commission know what use has been made of the research fund, and how does it rate its effectiveness?

Answer

The Commission thanks Ms Izquierdo Rojo for her question that gives the opportunity to give more information on the reform of the tobacco common market organization that the Commission presented to the Council and the European Parliament.

The Commission is fully aware of the social and economic importance of tobacco cultivation in Europe; this is why it has proposed to continue the tobacco common market organization, while allowing for a movement to higher quality cultivation, commanding abetter position in the market place.

The objective of the reform is indeed to preserve the employment linked with the cultivation of tobacco in Europe whilst increasing the quality of the production. In addition, the Commission recognizes the need to assist those who wish to leave this sector of production.

Concerning the Tobacco Research Fund, it is planned not only to continue its activities, but to provide it with increased resources by raising the percentage withheld from the premium to 2 %.

Question No 53 by Liam Hyland (H−0243/97)

Subject: REPS, Farm Retirement Scheme and Forestry Programme

Do sufficient financial resources exist to guarantee the continued funding of these three schemes for the remainder of this year and, furthermore, is the Commission committed to maintaining these schemes for the foreseeable future?

Answer

The Rural Environment Protection Scheme, the Farm Retirement Scheme and the Forestry Programme in Ireland are co‐financed from the budget of the EAGGF, Guarantee Section, within the framework of the accompanying measures.

The budget for 1997 contains sufficient funds for the accompanying measures throughout the whole of the Community. According to information currently available to the Commission, the authorized programmes can continue to operate in their present form until the end of the year. As with all agricultural spending, the Commission also monitors the relevant budget appropriations constantly. If there are any problems, it will ensure optimum distribution of available resources, in the context of the budgetary discipline that applies to all EAGGF expenditure.

In no case does the Commission intend to propose that these measures be halted. Thus, when preparing the preliminary draft of the budget plan for 1998, the Commission discussed the current situation regarding the implementation of the accompanying measures, using data supplied by Member States, in order to be able to take their requirements into account in its budget proposals.

Question No 54 by Jan Andersson (H−0250/97)

Subject: Tobacco‐growing subsidies

The EU spends large amounts on subsidizing tobacco‐growing in the Union, yet it is known that the use of tobacco causes major health problems and results inter alia in high medical care costs.

Does the Commission consider subsidies for tobacco‐growing to be compatible with sound European healthcare policy?

Answer

The Commission has always stressed the fact that aid to tobacco growers has no impact on the level of tobacco consumption in the European Union and the 'only' real effect of ending support for tobacco growers would be an increase in tobacco imports, as virtually the entire EU tobacco crop would be doomed if support were withdrawn.

I would like to stress that the Commission Report of 18 December last on the reorganization of the common market in tobacco goes some way to addressing the concerns expressed by the Honourable Member. In particular, the Commission has proposed increasing from 1 % to 2 % the levy on subsidies used to create the Community tobacco fund. This fund is devoted to public information projects on the dangers of smoking, particularly addressed to the young. It also finances research into less dangerous varieties of tobacco plant.

On the same occasion, the Commission also adopted a Communication on smoking prevention. This contains a series of policy options which could be taken at Community and Member State level to prevent smoking. The Parliament will of course have the possibility, in the near future, to express its views on this Communication.

Question No 55 by Marialiese Flemming (H−0259/97)

Subject: Animal transport

Is the Commission prepared to set up representatives of animal protection organizations that are organized as recognized associations in their own countries and have been carrying out their activities for longer than 5 years as monitors of the conditions in which animals are transported and to grant appropriate financial support?

Answer

The Commission believes that animal welfare organizations can play a valuable part in securing the correct observance of the requirements of the directive on the protection of animals during transport.

The Commission does already cooperate closely with Eurogroup for animal welfare, which represents the principal established welfare organizations in the Member States at the European level.

The responsibility for ensuring that livestock transporters respect the requirements of Directive 91/628/EEC, on the protection of animals during transport, rests primarily with the Member States.

However, under article 10 of this directive Commission experts may to the extent necessary for uniform application of this directive, carry out on the spot checks in collaboration with the competent authorities of the Member States.

The Commission doubts whether on the basis of this article it could insist on the participation of representatives of animal welfare societies in such control missions without the consent of the Member State concerned.

The Commission is, however, currently examining whether it would be possible to use properly qualified representatives of established animal welfare societies to verify the animal welfare situation in certain third countries of livestock transported to those countries from the Community.

Also subject to examination is the possibility of using part of the budgetary allocation granted for the control of slaughter animals to off‐set the costs of representatives of animal welfare societies participating in such third country monitoring.

Question No 56 by Mark Killilea (H−0264/97)

Subject: Cereals dispute settlement panel

In the light of the ongoing dispute between the EU, US and Canada concerning the management of agreed tariff quotas for certain cereals which were part of a trade compensation package following EU enlargement (Sweden, Finland and Austria), and the continued called by the US in particular for the establishment of a dispute settlement panel, can the Commission now state what the exact position of negotiations is, and whether threats of reprisals by the US in the form of resumption of its own Export Enhancement Programme could become a reality?

Answer

The Council has agreed a tariff quota for a certain quality of malting barley, subject to the withdrawal by the United States of its request for a panel on the Community's cereals and rice import regime. The Commission has also asked the management committee for its advice on a draft Commission regulation introducing a cumulative recovery system for rice subject to the same condition. The United States have, however, reiterated their request for a panel on the cereals and rice import regime. It remains to be seen whether this request will be withdrawn once the United States have completed their examination of the Community's barley and rice regulations.

The use by the United States of their export enhancement programme is subject to their WTO commitments.

Question No 57 by Sirkka‐Liisa Anttila (H−0266/97)

Subject: Differences in conditions between regions within the EU in mountainous areas and in the North to be taken into account in agricultural policy

A farm is a business and agriculture is a livelihood which it should be possible to pursue profitably. The operating environment of agriculture, and in particular the conditions in the various parts of the Union, differ very widely. In the Union's best production areas it is possible to produce competitively and efficiently, whereas in the South, the North and in mountainous areas the differences from Central Europe in terms of productivity are particularly great as a result of climatic conditions, greater distances etc.

One of the main objectives of the EU is to narrow the differences in terms of production between the different regions. If nothing is done to compensate for these differences, agriculture in the Union's northern regions, and in the southern region which suffers from drought, will wither away. This regional difference in conditions requires special measures to be permanently in place. One of the most effective ways would be an agricultural policy offering several options and possibilities, which would take account in an equitable manner of these differences and differing needs.

How does the Commission intend to take account in preparing its own agricultural policy reforms, of the special needs I have described, and by what means can a solution be found to the continuing competitive disadvantages of farmers particularly in the extreme north and in mountainous areas?

Answer

A wide range of Community measures already exists in order to compensate for the permanent competitive disadvantage of farmers in the Community's less‐favoured mountainous and outlying regions. Examples are the Structural Fund measures, implemented in the framework of the Objective 5a sectorial measures (modernisation of agricultural structures, including direct payments to farms situated in mountainous or less‐favoured regions) and in the framework of regional measures (under Objective 1 for regions lagging behind in development, under Objective 5b for the development of rural areas and under Objective 6 for arctic regions). Certain agricultural common market organizations have also introduced measures, or made special arrangements, for farmers situated in less‐favoured regions.

The concerns expressed by the Honourable Member also relate to the current debate on the present results of and future prospects for the common agricultural policy and structural policy. Other questions, such as those relating to Community enlargement, are also receiving attention. The Commission held a European conference on rural development last November, in Cork. In 1996 it adopted the first report on economic and social cohesion in the Union. This report deals specifically with the impact of structural or sectorial policy – such as the CAP – on cohesion during the period 1989‐1999. These questions will be raised at the European Forum on cohesion to be held at the end of April in Brussels.

In any event, the Commission will be presenting the general direction of policy and initial proposals in the framework of the ‚Santer Package’ within six months following the conclusion of the Intergovernmental Conference in Amsterdam.

Question No 58 by Josu Imaz San Miguel (H−0267/97)

Subject: COM in the milk sector

The Commission is drawing up a proposal for a COM in the milk sector. At present the quota allocated to some Member States does not cover internal consumption, which obliges them to turn to the external market to meet growing internal demand. In the mountain areas of these countries, which are highly specialized in milk production, social problems are created because it is difficult to find alternative economic activities and employment. Furthermore, in these areas stockbreeders are making efforts to conserve the rural environment.

Is the Commission considering increasing the milk quota for these countries in its forthcoming reform? Does it agree that the income of these stock‐breeders must be supplemented with a system of direct aid, adjusted in favour of mountain areas, in accordance with the spirit of the Cork Declaration?

Answer

The quotas for Member States were set in 1994, on the basis of production at that time. Any other method of allocation, particularly in relation to the population or national consumption of individual countries, would have made any agreement on the scheme impossible, or would have led to the total quantity set for the Community being far too high to enable the scheme"s objective to be achieved. This objective was to keep EU milk production within reasonable limits.

The Commission is currently analysing developments in the market for milk and milk products. The results will shortly be made public, so that all the parties involved can participate in the discussions. This will result in the Council"s determining the policy for the next few years.

Question No 60 by Leen van der Waal (H−0167/97)

Subject: Negotiations with Norway on an increase in the TAC of plaice

When the TACs for 1997 were being fixed, the Commission gave an assurance that it would try to reach agreement with Norway on an increase in the plaice catch from 1 February 1997. We understand that it has not yet succeeded.

Why did the Commission not complete the negotiations with Norway by 1 February 1997? Has the Commission completed the discussions in the meantime? If so, what was the outcome? If not, when can an explanation be expected?

Answer

The Commission undertook to make contact with Norway before February 1997 with the view to exploiting possibilities for an increase in the 1997 TAC for plaice agreed between the Parties in December 1996.

Norway responded positively to the request and said it could, in these circumstances, accept a small increase.

Against this background the Commission has suggested an increase of the TAC by 3, 000 tonnes to a total of 80, 000 tonnes. It has learned in the meantime that this increase will be officially confirmed shortly by Norway.

Question No 61 by Doeke Eisma (H−0196/97)

Subject: Preparations for meetings in the context of the Berne Convention

All the Member States of the EU, and the EU as such, are members of the Standing Committee of the Berne Convention. They have between them 15 of the total of 34 votes. Since some decisions require a two‐thirds majority, the EU can exert a veto. The Commission appears to have been badly prepared for the meeting in December 1996.

Can the Commission explain what is done, both within the Commission and between the Commission and the Member States, in the way of preparatory work for meetings of the Standing Committee?

Does the Commission intend to improve its preparatory work for these meetings? If so, how?

Answer

At the beginning of October the Commission received, from the Berne Convention secretariat, the proposals concerning amendments to Annexes I, II and III of the Convention, in the framework of the Standing Committee.

For those matters within the Community's field of competence (in particular the amendment to Annex III), the internal consultation was initiated with the responsible services on 11 October 1996, ending on 31 October 1996.

The written procedure concerning the Commission's draft recommendation to the Council began on 5 November 1996 and ended on 14 November 1996. The recommendation was submitted on 15 November 1996. The Council did not grant the Commission a mandate before the meeting of the Standing Committee or during the consultations on the spot. The Commission was therefore unable to express an opinion. Moreover, the amendment to Annex III was postponed by the Committee until its meeting of December 1997.

In the field of competence shared with the Member States (covering many of the points proposed for the amendments to Annexes I and II), the Commission attended coordination meetings at the time of the Sanding Committee meeting. The first vote by the contracting parties on the amendments to Annexes I and II proposed by Monaco failed to achieve the necessary majority.

This was then the subject of a second vote when the Standing Committee finally decided to accept this proposal.

Within the given time limits, the Commission carried out the preparatory work for the Standing Committee, strictly respecting the procedures. The draft proposals were submitted to the Council which had been informally notified on 31 October. The Commission cannot take the place of the Member States in adopting common positions in areas within the Community's field of competence.

In order to improve preparations for Standing Committee meetings, the Convention secretariat will be asked to transmit the documents at an earlier date. It should be emphasized in this respect that this year the Convention secretariat, which is aware of Community decision‐making procedures, notified the Commission as early as March of proposals to be submitted to the Standing Committee in December 1997.

Subject: Reduction in biological diversity

EU aid for the protection of landscapes has, owing to the way in which the rules are formulated, resulted in less being done to promote biological diversity.

Is the Commission prepared to look into this problem and revise the rules?

The Honourable Member's question is not precise enough for the Commission to give an appropriate answer.

Question No 63 by Yiannis Roubatis (H−0201/97)

Subject: Participation by Turkish Cypriot representatives in negotiations for the accession of Cyprus

At the last meeting of the Council of Foreign Ministers in Brussels, the British, German and French Foreign Ministers requested the inclusion in the text of the common position of a proposal for the participation of the Turkish Cypriot authorities in negotiations for the accession of Cyprus. However, it is generally known that the European Union only acknowledges the legal government of Cyprus as being entitled to participate in negotiations for the accession of Cyprus.

Does the Commission intend to recognize as a legitimate interlocutor the self‐styled 'Turkish‐Cypriot government, ' which is illegal and not recognized by the international community thereby undermining the exclusive right of the Government of the Cypriot Republic to conduct negotiations for the accession of Cyprus?

Does the Commission not consider that the participation of the Turkish Cypriots in the negotiations would put Turkey in a position to dominate the accession procedure at a time of crisis, not only in Cyprus itself but also in relations between Greece and Turkey?

Does the Commission consider that changes in the agreed accession procedure for Cyprus might also compromise proceedings for enlargement to include countries of Central and Eastern Europe?

Answer

Negotiations with Cyprus for EU membership will commence six months after the end of the Intergovernmental Conference, the results of which will be taken into account. The Commission member with responsibility for this matter confirmed this undertaking on the part of the European Union during his visit to Cyprus at the end of February. In accordance with the United Nations Resolutions, EU Member States recognize a single legitimate government in Cyprus.

During the preparatory debates for the structured dialogue meeting which should have been held on 25 February 1997, many Member States wanted the Union's common position to express the wish for the Turkish Cypriot community to be involved in the negotiating process, i.e. for progress towards a negotiated settlement of the Cyprus question to be sufficiently advanced to permit such a scenario. It did not prove possible to reach agreement on the formulation of a statement to this effect.

Question No 64 by Roy Perry (H−0204/97)

Subject: Personnel/financial control

On what basis has the Commission refused to lift the diplomatic immunity of certain former officials within DG XXIII?

Answer

The Commission lifted the ‚duty of reserve’ of 24 officials, thus making possible their interrogation by the Belgian authorities, and the immunity of 3 members of its staff. In relation to the persons (two former officials and an official) to whom the honourable member may refer, the Commission lifted the ‚duty of reserve’ in February 1995, November 1995 and April 1996 respectively. A request for the lifting of immunity was examined by the Commission in June 1996 but, as it contained no information or justification of the demand, the Commission asked for clarification. Some clarification was given on 30 July, but in the case of one official and one former official, despite the long period following the lifting of the ‚duty or reserve’ and their readiness to answer questions, there had been no contacts with them from the Belgian authorities nor any request to provide an explanation. It appeared to the Commission that if the Belgian authorities requested from these officials such explanations, the alleged problems could be rapidly eliminated from the inquiry. The Commission understands that this is in fact still the situation.

Question No 65 by Ilona Graenitz (H−0206/97)

Subject: CFC smuggling

According to reports in a German news magazine, substances which are prohibited under the Montreal Protocol (chlorofluorocarbons) are being smuggled into the Member States of the European Union.

Has this claim been verified by the Commission?

If so, what measures has it taken, or can it take, to prevent this smuggling?

Answer

The European Commission takes very seriously claims of illegal trade in ozone depleting substances. For example, under the terms of Regulation (EC) 1468/81 on mutual assistance between the administrative authorities of the MemberStates and cooperation between the latter and the Commission to ensure the correct application of the law on Customs or Agricultural matters, the Commission requested information from all Member States Customs authorities in 1996. Further enquiries are currently underway and the Commission intends to request the Customs Authorities in all Member States to take action, including legal action, to ensure better awareness of the controls on trade in ozone‐depleting substances, and the sanctions faced by those who are convicted of illegal trade. In the meantime, the Commission has made clear that anyone with specific evidence of illegal trade should make it available so that the competent authorities can take the appropriate action.

The Commission and the MemberStaets have been aware of the rumours of illegal trade for some time, and an ad hoc group has been established to identify possible sources of illegal imports. It includes representatives from the Commission, environment and customs authorities of the Member States and representatives of the European chemical industries. The group has reviewed current control measures and, where potential weaknesses have b een identified, suggested ways of tightening them up.

Discussions are currently underway in the context of the revision of Regulation 3093/94 on substances that deplete the ozone layer about how to best strengthen the control system. Through the quota and subsequent licensing system the Commission already exercises a high level of control on the imports of controlled substances into the Community. Further, given that a coordinated effort by all Parties is necessary to address the risk posed by illegal trade in CFCs, the European Community is pressing all Parties to the Montreal Protocol to introduce efficient monitoring for imports and exports of ozone depleting substances, including an effective licensing system.

***

Question No 66 by Per Stenmarck (H−0207/97)

Subject: The impact of cars on the environment

Finding a solution to Europe's environmental problems is one of the biggest challenges facing the EU. Cars undeniably account for a large proportion of the emissions which enter the environment. Europe's car industry is conducting extensive research into replacing current engine types with batteries or hybrid engines so as to minimize emissions.

Is the Commission prepared to submit a long‐range proposal aimed at minimizing environmentally harmful emissions from cars?

Answer

Pollution caused by cars is a major problem which the European Community has determined to reduce in the framework of its responsibilities which it shares with the national and local authorities and the citizens themselves.

Over recent years the Commission has set up a multi‐phase strategy covering the short, medium and long term.

For the short term, in June 1996 the Commission proposed a directive designed to reduce emissions by new vehicles from the year 2000. This directive is part of an integrated approach which takes account of the ’Auto/oil’ programme which is scheduled to come before the European Parliament at first reading during the current part‐session.

The proposal concerning motor vehicles includes:

‐ standards to be met in the year 2000;

‐ additional measures, such as a compulsory OBD system.

At the same time, the Commission also made a proposal designed to improve fuels. This proposes to introduce:

‐ harmonized specifications for ‚petrol’ and ‚gas oil’ fuels in the year 2000;

‐ a procedure giving Member States the option of introducing ‚special fuels’ which would be more ecological.

For the medium term, in its proposal on vehicle emissions the Commission invited the European Parliament and Council to adopt stringent indicative limits reflecting what will be technologically possible by the year 2005 and for which Member States would have the possibility of granting tax incentives. These target values for the year 2005 are intended to notify the industry that it must sustain its research and development efforts in order to develop solutions which are more favourable to the environment and applicable to the total vehicle fleet.

These values are based on estimated developments in ‚conventional’ technologies, i.e. technologies which are based on the use of the thermal petrol, gas oil or even gas engine.

They must be confirmed or revised following an additional evaluation programme as described in the proposal and which, in particular, must make it possible to better specify the desired characteristics of fuels.

In the long term, non‐conventional technologies – such as electric or hybrid vehicles, or even fuel cell vehicles – must be the subject of intensive research as they offer great potential for the future. In particular, there is a need to reduce the costs of these technologies to the consumer and improve technical aspects such as the density of energy stored in batteries or recharging time. In 1995, the Commission decided to create the ‚Car of the Future’ Task Force with the aim of making a more concentrated use of its research resources and coordinating them with the considerable activity already carried out nationally or by the industry itself.

This Task Force is now operational. It not only brings together research experts, but also all the services responsible for environmental protection or safety, as well as the use of advanced technologies such as telematics which could influence technical trends for the vehicles of the future. The next research programme will further supplement its activities.

Vehicles fitted with non‐conventional technologies are being encouraged by the public, Community and national authorities. They do not at present amount to a market of any significant size, and the variable emergence of these technologies is not expected before 2005.

***

Question No 67 by Sir Jack Stewart‐clark (H−0208/97)

Subject: Animal experimentation

In the 5th Environmental Action Programme the Commission has accepted the need to reduce animal use in experimentation. Without proper statistics from Member States any reduction is impossible to quantify. Under the Council of Europe's procedure for multilateral consultation, many countries of the Council of Europe, including all Member States of the EU, agreed a formula for obtaining and recording statistics. On 27 May 1994 the Commission published the results of those endeavours. Why are the Member States still not producing proper annual statistics as required under the Directive and why has the Commission not taken any action to accelerate this process?

Answer

The Directive 86/609/EEC on protection of animals used for experimental and other scientific purposes requires that the Commission publishes a report on statistics on the use of laboratory animals. This is to be done on the basis of information from the Member States. The Commission is dependent on the Member States to provide the data but the Directive fails to specify the level of detail of the information to be supplied. For example if a Member State decided to submit only one page with minimal information the Commission has no formal means to require more.

The first report was published by the Commission in 1994 with difficulty as the data submitted by the Member States were not comparable.

At the present time, the only means to improve this situation is to achieve a ‚gentleman's agreement’ between the Member States on the level of data to be supplied.

The Commission has already made substantial efforts into developing a standard format for the statistics. The statistical tables which had been developed together with experts from the Member States were, however, not acceptable to many Competent Authorities. The tables were considered to need simplification and alignment with the requirements contained in the Council of Europe Convention ETS 123 for the protection of vertebrate animals used for experimental and other scientific purposes.

A renewed attempt was made in February 1997 when a group of experts prepared a Proposal which hopefully will be agreed by the Competent Authorities at their next meeting in April 1997. When all the Member States will have implemented the proposed format, it will be possible to collect comparable data. The first full year when this new format could be used is 1998.

Question No 68 by Peter Truscott (H−0212/97)

Subject: Outbreak of cryptosporidium in the UK

Following the recent serious outbreak of cryptosporidium in Hertfordshire and north London in the UK, affecting the drinking water of 680 000 water consumers, causing cases of sickness and diarrhoea, will the Commission consider implementing minimum standards for drinking water which would embrace the microscopic parasite, cryptosporidium (an organism found in farm and domestic animals), investigate whether the occurrence of cryptosporidium contamination of water supplies is on the increase, and whether it constitutes a serious threat to public health throughout Europe and recommend fines against water companies found to be negligent in such cases, and compensation for affected consumers?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the recent cryptosporidium outbreak in the North London area of the UK.

The current Drinking Water Directive (80/778/EEC) does not include a standard for cryptosporidium in drinking water, but there is a general obligation for Member States to ensure that drinking water ‚should not contain any pathogenic organisms’. This obligation is taken further in the Proposed Revision of the Directive (COM(94)612 final), which was considered by the European Parliament at its December 1996 plenary session.

The Proposal stipulates in Article 4(1) b, that ‚Member States shall take the measures necessary to ensure that water intended for human consumption is free of pathogenic micro‐organism and parasites in numbers which constitute a potential danger to human health’. This includes, inter alia, the parasite cryptosporidium, and therefore, under the Proposal, Member States will have to ensure that water supplies are free of cryptosporidium.

The sampling and measurement of cryptosporidium is a slow and laborious process, and currently requires a huge volume of water (over one hundred litres) to ensure the test is carried out properly. However, in the wake of several recent outbreaks in Europe and elsewhere, there is a great deal of research work being carried out to develop better and more efficient means of testing for cryptosporidium. This includes work being undertaken by the World Health Organization which is reviewing the need for a Guideline value for cryptosporidium in its ‚Guidelines for Drinking Water Quality’. The Commission is actively involved in the review process, and will assess the implications of the WHO review after the revised Guidelines have been adopted at the end of 1997.

With respect to Community research on this subject, the Commission is currently considering undertaking a specific study on cryptosporidium as part of the Standards Measurements and Testing research programme. The Commission will also give every consideration for the funding of other proposals on this topic which meet the necessary criteria for quality and relevance under other relevant programmes of the Fourth Framework Programme on Research.

With respect to the possibility for the Commission to recommend fines against water companies found to be negligent in such cases, and compensation for affected customs, this is a matter for the competent national authorities to decide on, and not the Commission.

Question No 69 by Gianfranco Dell'alba (H−0222/97)

Subject: Plan by the Italian Government to incorporate the 'Golden Share' principle into the STET statute

The Italian Ministry of Finance has announced its intention to amend the STET statute by the end of March in order to incorporate the 'Golden Share' provided for in Law 474/94. Some time ago (in the ENI case) the Commission pointed out to Italy that such legislation was incompatible with Community laws on the free movement of capital and the freedom of business establishment. Several months ago the Commissioner responsible for the internal market, Mario Monti, announced his intention of proposing to the Commission the text of a communication to the Member States which would draw attention to this incompatibility and suggest action to be taken to avoid it.

What is the Commission waiting for in order to issue the communication or, at least, formally to open an infringement procedure, so as to make it quite clear to the governments concerned that, unless they amend their laws, they will be brought before the Court of Justice?

Answer

The privatization of a company is an economic policy decision and as such lies exclusively within the field of competence of the Member States, by virtue of the principle of neutrality in regard to the system of property ownership laid down in Article 222 of the Treaty.

However, in its institutional role, the Commission follows the development of privatizations with the greatest interest. It does so both in the context of the Community's general economic policy and from the point of view of safeguarding the legal framework which governs the economic choices of Member States, and thus the coherent and uniform application of Community law in this area and, in particular, provisions concerning freedom of establishment (Art. 52) and the free movement of capital (Art. 73B).

A global assessment of the legislation in the various Member States in this area is currently in progress; this could very soon result in a communication to the Member States.

The European Parliament will of course be informed of this.

Question No 70 by Inger Schörling (H−0227/97)

Subject: Wolves

Is the Commission aware that the Swedish Environmental Protection Agency allows the hunting of predatory animals protected under the 1992 Habitats Directive, in order to safeguard their prey?

It was decided in 1996 to cull 98 lynx. The Environmental Protection Agency is reportedly set to take a decision next week on an application by the Governor of Jämtland for permission to shoot the last remaining four wolves in that county as well.

These animals are in danger of extinction throughout Europe, and so are not merely an internal Swedish concern. What action does the Commission intend to take?

Answer

The Commission will examine the report on derogations presented by Sweden in accordance with Article 16 of the Directive 92/43/EEC on the conservation of natural habitats and of wild fauna and flora.

Following the rules established by this directive the Commission shall give its opinion on these derogations within 12 months following receipt of the report.

The report of Sweden covering the period 1995/1996 is scheduled to be transmitted within a few weeks.

However, the Commission has asked the Swedish authorities for information in advance of the report regarding the facts referred to by the Honourable Member. The Commission will keep her informed of its findings.

Question No 71 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0228/97)

Subject: Lindholm report on equal treatment under the EC Staff Regulations

In February, the European Parliament adopted by a convincing majority a number of amendments to the Commission proposal amending the Staff Regulations of officials and the Conditions of Employment of other servants of the European Communities in respect of equal treatment. As rapporteur, it is my duty to monitor what subsequently becomes of the proposal. What action has the Commission taken to incorporate Parliament's views in its proposal and, more particularly, have the other institutions concerned been consulted pursuant to Article 24 of the Treaty establishing a Single Council and a Single Commission of the European Communities?

Answer

At the European Parliament's plenary session of 19 February 1997, the Commission adopted a position on Mrs Lindholm's report, setting out the arguments in favour of a rapid approval of the Commission proposal amending the Staff Regulations of officials and other servants of the European Communities. The Commission does not believe it is appropriate to call into question this proposal – the result of four years of reflection – at the present point in time. It should be supported so that the principles of equal treatment and non‐discrimination which it contains can be finally recognized.

That said, the Commission envisages, when re‐examining Staff Regulations as a whole – a process which will necessarily involve lengthy internal negotiation procedures and consultations with all the institutions – to take account of a number of important amendments adopted by the European Parliament on which it is agreed in principle.

Article 24 of the Treaty, which establishes a single Council and Commission of the European Communities, and which states that the Council shall, acting by a qualified majority on a proposal from the Commission and after consulting the other institutions concerned, lay down the Staff Regulations of officials of the European Communities and the Conditions of Employment of other servants of those Communities, constitutes the legal basis for the Commission's proposed change to the Staff Regulations. It is up to the Council to gather the opinions of all the interested institutions before deciding.

Question No 72 by Roberto Speciale (H−0232/97)

Subject: The plight of children in Romania

In view of the serious plight (well documented by the international press) of children in Romania, many of whom are living below the threshold of poverty, with an increasing number living on the streets, and in view of the European Parliament's resolution of 12 December 1996 on Romania, what representations does the Commission intend to make to ensure that the Romanian Government fulfils its undertakings to improve the situation of children, and what steps does the Commission intend to take to adopt, in conjunction with the Council, the Member States and the Romanian Government, a programme to protect children and a specific programme for street children in the principal cities of Romania?

What progress has been made in implementing the specific support granted to the Romanian Government under the PHARE programme for developing a programme to protect children?

Answer

The Commission attaches very great importance to improving the situation of abandoned children in Romania.

For a number of years now, the Commission has been working in close cooperation with the Romanian Government and the relevant NGOs in support of several aid projects, in particular at orphanages.

The Commission has provided for an increase in its assistance and the inclusion of projects to help street children as part of the Romanian section of its 1996‐1999 multi‐annual PHARE programme. This new programme will start on the expiry of the present child protection project, amounting to ECU 12 million and which expires in August 1997.

In addition, the Commission and Romanian Government have reached agreement on the allocation of 3, 450 billion lei from the counterpart fund for child protection. The Commission and Romanian Government are currently looking at the possibility of increasing the amount granted to child protection projects through this counterpart fund.

Question No 73 by David Martin (H−0234/97)

Subject: Humanitarian action in Bulgaria

What action is the Commission taking to address the deep social and economic crisis in Bulgaria, where the average monthly income in the country has fallen from $100 – $130 at the beginning of 1996 to $2 – $7 in January of this year?

Answer

The Commission is very much concerned about the socio‐economic crisis in Bulgaria, a country which is closely linked to the Community via an Association Agreement and which has applied for membership.

In order to demonstrate its commitment towards Bulgaria and its solidarity with the Bulgarian population, the Community has provided and is providing assistance is a number of areas to Bulgaria.

Over the past six months the Community has mobilized ECU 83.6 million worth of assistance including macro‐financial assistance and energy assistance to promote the necessary decisions by Bulgaria on economic and structural reforms as well as emergency social assistance to respond to the socioeconomic crisis.

A 20 Mecu Emergency Social Assistance Scheme (Phare), has been set up which provides income support to retired people and those dependent on welfare, particularly the sick, the disabled and the orphans. Payments under this scheme are being made to 500, 000 needy households and 500 social institutions beginning in the week of 24/03/97 through Bulgaria's welfare system.

In addition, the Commission is providing emergency assistance of in total 1.1 MECU, consisting of a grant for food (0.5 MECU) to the most needy of the population and medical supplies (0.6 MECU) to hospitals via NGOs. Should the humanitarian situation in the country deteriorate further, the Commission could envisage additional actions in Bulgaria.

The Community has also helped to bridge acute cash difficulties facing private farmers by softening the lending terms of a 7 MECU agricultural credit line to promote planting for the 1997 harvest. Likewise, a reduction of 20 MECU was agreed of the Bulgarian co‐financing to the Phare Cross‐border Co‐operation Programme in order to progress under projects which were blocked due to the government's budgetary problems.

In order to underline the Community's solidarity and to enhance political, economic and social assistance in Bulgaria, the Community is preparing the following forms of further assistance:

Following the agreement reached between Bulgaria and the IMF, the Commission is preparing further substantial Community macro‐financial assistance on the basis of a credible economic stabilization and reform programme. The Community macro‐financial assistance will complement funding for Bulgaria from IMF, worth $ 659 Mio., which includes also a $ 148 Mio. assistance under the compensatory and contingency facility to finance grain imports to ease shortages until the next harvest.

Following the Bulgarian request for assistance, the Commission has started to mobilize support for Bulgaria from the G24 group of industrialized countries and asked them to make specific assistance pledges.

In addition, the Commission Phare programme is being mobilized to help implement the necessary reforms in key sectors such as privatization, banking, agriculture and energy. Already, the Phare programme has spent 540 MECU during the period 1990‐1996. Loans from the European Investment Bank amount to 286 MECU.

Moreover, additional social assistance will be provided, on top of the 20 MECU already allocated, to the most needy in the society, e.g. pensioners and persons dependent on welfare, in order to alleviate the initial social cost of further price liberalization.

These steps should help to establish the conditions for economic recovery and strengthen public support for reform.

Question No 74 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0236/97)

Subject: Placing of committee documents on the Internet

The activities of EU committees have attracted increasing attention in the European media. It is estimated that 424 committees every year take more than 5000 decisions, many of which have turned out to be highly significant for members of the public. The committees' activities, however, are far from transparent, and the agendas and minutes of their meetings are not published.

One solution which might enable the public to gain greater insight would be to place all Commission management committee documents on the Internet. Relatively simple programs would then enable the public to monitor the progress of proposals from inception to adoption.

Is the Commission prepared to place all committee documents on the Internet?

Answer

The Commission would inform the Honourable Member that the committees in question do not take decisions but issue opinions, further to which the Commission then takes decisions.

The proceedings of these committees are not made public. The Commission would also remind you that, in accordance with the procedures laid down under the PLUMB‐DELORS, KLEPSCH‐DELORS, and KLEPSCH‐MILLAN agreements, it informs the European Parliament of the modus vivendi and undertakings given by the Commission before the Budget Committee in September 1996.

As a rule, the Commission's decisions are published in the Official Journal of the European Communities, L series.

The texts of acts published in the OJ are also loaded into the CELEX data base. It will soon be possible to search the CELEX texts via the Internet. Also, the Office of Publications is preparing a new system (EUDOR), also accessible through the Internet, which will make it possible to download the exact images of the pages published in the Official Journal on the basis of the act references.

Question No 75 by Pat Gallagher (H−0242/97)

Subject: Compensation for Irish fishermen

The fishing industry and the fish‐processing industry in Ireland have been severely hit by the storms of recent months. Will the Commission now bring forward an urgent compensation package to assist fishermen and those involved in the fish‐processing industry affected by these severe storms?

Answer

Community regulations(1) in certain circumstances provide for Member States to take measures for the temporary cessation of fishing vessels.

FIFG assistance may be used only to finance measures intended to offset partially the loss of income suffered as a result of temporary cessation of fishing activities caused by unforeseen and non‐repetitive events resulting from biological phenomena in particular.

Information available to the Commission indicates that the necessary conditions mentioned above have not been fulfilled.

Question No 76 by James Fitzsimons (H−0244/97)

Subject: European tax on petrol, diesel, electricity, natural gas and coal

What, if any, percentage increases on present prices to Irish consumers of petrol, diesel, electricity, natural gas and coal will be provoked by the full imposition of the Commission's recent proposals for minimum tax rates on energy products?

Answer

The Commission has evaluated the impact, in all the Member States, of its proposal for a directive on the consumer price of lead‐free petrol and diesel fuel. These products were selected because they are everyday consumables which account for a not inconsiderable proportion of household expenditure and also because it is on these products that the increases in Community minimum tax rates scheduled for 1998 will have the greatest impact.

As regards Ireland, the Commission calculates that the impact of the proposal for a directive on the pump prices of these two fuels will be nil. According to the assumptions made by the Commission, the tax rates applied by Ireland on 1 January 1998 will be higher than the Community minimum tax rates.

As regards electricity, natural gas and coal, Ireland does not currently apply any tax to these products. Accordingly, if the proposal is adopted, from 1 January 1998 they will have to bear a new tax at least equal to the minimum rates laid down in the proposal for a directive. The impact of the proposal for a directive on the consumer prices of these products has not been calculated in the studies carried out by the Commission. However, since the Community minimum tax rates for electricity, natural gas and coal have been fixed at a very low level, the impact should be very limited.

Question No 77 by Brian Crowley (H−0248/97)

Subject: College of Commissioners and the Member State

What justification can the Commission offer for its proposal contained in its communication to the IGC on the 'composition, organization and working of the Commission' to limit the total number of Commissioners to 20 and furthermore, has the Commission considered the necessity of maintaining the political legitimacy of the College in the eyes of the European electorate by maintaining the principle of one Commissioner per Member State regardless of the number of Member States?

Answer

As the Commission indicated in the communication to which the Honourable Member refers, the composition of the Commission must simultaneously respect the principles of legitimacy and efficiency.

To ensure compliance with those two principles, as well as with those of the collegiate nature and independence of the Commission, the number of Commissioners would have to be reduced to one per Member State.

At the same time, and for the same reasons, an appropriate procedure will have to be devised so that the composition of the Commission could be reviewed if the number of Member States exceeded a certain figure. The Commission suggests that there should be no more than 20 Commissioners.

That procedure should provide for a balance between the Member States.

The Commission takes the view that its legitimacy and efficiency would be maintained in this way.

Question No 78 by Helena Torres Marques (H−0251/97)

Subject: NOW programme authorizations and payments

The rate of utilization of the 1995 budget appropriations relating to women and/or equal opportunities for men and women was generally high, except in the case of the NOW (New Opportunities for Women) programme. The apparent reason for the lower utilization of the NOW appropriations is not the lack of applications to finance but the lack of co‐funding from the governments of the Member States.

Could the Commission supply data for each Member State relating to the way in which NOW programme authorizations and payments were dealt with?

Answer

In most Member States the launch of the Employment Community Initiative (including the NOW strand) was delayed, and most projects selected only began their activities in 1995.

The estimated payments for that year, as laid out in the financial plans prepared by the Member States, were therefore optimistic, and actual consumption of finance was lower than estimated.

In Member States operating an annual commitment system (7 out of 15) the system of payment is as follows:

‐ a first payment of 50 % of the amount for the year, as set out in the Decision, is made automatically following the adoption of the Commission Decision;

‐ following the provision by the Member State of certification that 50 % of that first payment has actually been consumed by final beneficiaries, a second payment of 30 % of the annual amount is made;

‐ the final 20 % is paid either at the end of the year or following the provision by the Member State of certification that the amount has actually been spent by the final beneficiaries.

The Community funding that had been provided in 1994, but not used in that year as a result of the late start of the programmes in most Member States, was transferred to the 1995 year, through modifications by the Member States of their financial plans.

For the Member States operating a single commitment system (8 out of 15), the first payment that is made following the Commission Decision on the Operational Programme of that Member State amounts to 50 % of the total funding, if the amount of the expenditure estimated over the first half of the programme warrants it. If this is not the case, the initial advance is limited to 30 %. The payment of the second advance and the final claim follow the rules mentioned above over the lifetime of the programme. Initial payments were made in 1994, and no Member State had exhausted this funding during 1995; thus no requests for a second advance from Member States operating a single commitment system was received in 1995.

At the meeting of the ESF Committee on the Community Initiatives on 21 March 1997, all Member States confirmed that there were no difficulties concerning the national co‐financing of the Employment Community Initiative.

Question No 79 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0254/97)

Subject: Insurance Companies' Reserve Fund in Greece

Over the last two years the cancellation of the licences of insurance companies has caused serious problems for the reserve fund of the private automobile insurance sector: a deficit of Drs. 25 bn. has appeared in the Insurance Companies' Subsidiary Fund.

Given the widespread concern that other insurance companies may withdraw from the market, thereby increasing the deficit in the reserve fund, will the Commission say whether it considers legitimate and legally valid, from the point of view of the interests of policy holders, the view that insurance premiums should be raised again in order to cover deficits in the reserve fund?

Answer

Under the system introduced by Directive 92/49/EEC (third directive on non‐life insurance), which applies to car insurance, it is the responsibility of the competent authorities in the Member State of origin to monitor insurance companies to ensure that they are financially sound, in particular in regard to solvency and the setting up of sufficient reserve funds. The same directive also defines the minimum standards governing the setting up of reserve funds.

Financial control of Greek companies with their head office in Greece is exclusively a matter for the Greek authorities. In other words, the accounting practices relating to the premiums policy of Greek insurance companies, which permits them to set up sufficient reserve funds to respect their contractual undertakings, must be viewed in the light of Greek law and is the responsibility of the supervisory body of the Greek Ministry of Trade.

Question No 80 by Clive Needle (H−0269/97)

Subject: Flood prevention measures

The Happisburgh‐Winterton area in Norfolk is subject to severe flooding every year, causing damage to the local environment and great hardship to the lives of local people.

The Commission is in the process of conducting a demonstration programme on the integrated management of coastal zones. Can it indicate when the programme will be concluded and whether funding for preventive measures against flooding is likely to be available?

Answer

The demonstration programme on the integrated management of coastal zones will be concluded by the end of the year 1998.

The aim of the programme is not to address specific local situations but to find the organizational and structural deficiencies leading to the degradation of the environment in the coastal zones of the Community.

The programme will address in particular the lack of coordination between the various levels of responsibility at national, local and European level and envisage strategies to correct the existing problems.

Question No 81 by Alan Donnelly (H−0270/97)

Subject: Port state controls information

The Port State Control Directive 95/21/EC(2) was an important step towards the elimination of substandard and unsafe ships from European waters. Can the Commission now explain if and how the Paris MOU on PSC and its Secretariat are cooperating with the Commission to increase the scope of information collected so the standards set by flag states, ship owners, operators and ports can be properly monitored; to ensure that this information is fully accessible to those making commercial decisions on chartering, purchasing and insuring ships and those monitoring compliance with statutory and classification quality standards; and to include appropriate information from classification societies and survey organizations; to ensure, with the help of Member State administrations, that the improved database is fully available to the Commission and Parliament so they can independently review the performance of the organizations involved and the penalties imposed for non‐compliance?

Answer

Following the entry into force of Directive 95/21/EC on port State control (PSC), changes in the management structure under the Paris Memorandum of Understanding to streamline policy‐making and to focus the available resources have proved to be successful in terms of improved cooperation between the Commission, the MOU participants and the MOU Secretariat.

The Directive has necessitated certain modifications to the co‐called SIRENAC database to which all PSE inspections within the European region are reported and the Commission works actively with the Paris MOU to implement these improvements (which are partly financed by the Commission) and to develop an amplified database which will expand and facilitate analysis of data. These activities facilitate the more effective application of the Directive.

At the same time, the Commission attaches great importance to the fact that the recorded results of port State control inspections are made easily accessible to the public so that all participants in shipping activities can make appropriate use of the information in their commercial decision‐making. It is understood that by the end of this year, the quarterly lists of detained vessels under the Paris MOU will be made available on the Internet. That is a useful advance. It is believed it should go further since the maritime industry and the general public, as well as the Commission and this Parliament, have a vested interest in prompt, reliable and precise information about the quality of vessels operating in European waters.

The need for additional or more timely information is therefore kept under constant review and initiatives are taken accordingly. With specific reference to the classification societies, at the forthcoming PSC Committee meeting the Commission will propose a refined and harmonized system for accurately determining classification societies' responsibility for deficiencies leading to detention of a vessel. It is hoped that the honourable Member will share the belief that implementation of this proposal will contribute significantly to a fair and practical assessment of classification societies' performance.

It is clear from all of this that the Commission pursues a proactive policy in the development and utilization of PSC data in efforts to ensure full and effective transparency and, consequently, beneficial effects on the safety of shipping.

Subject: Town‐twinning schemes

What proportion of total EU resources is earmarked annually to foster twinning activities in the EU?

1991: ECU 3.00 million
1992: ECU 3.55 million

1994: ECU 3.20 million
1995: ECU 3.50 million
1996: ECU 7.50 million
1997: ECU 7.50 million

The report for the year 1995 was sent to the European Parliament on 24 July 1996. The report for the year 1996 is currently being prepared, and will be sent to the European Parliament when it has been completed.

The funds for administrative expenditure in this area are included in the administration budget of the European Commission, but are not shown separately.
