Question No 83 by Karin Riis‐jørgensen (H−0278/97)

Subject: Duty on goods imported into the EU on GSP certificates

The Member States' customs authorities are currently reclaiming large sums in respect of duties on goods imported into the EU on GSP certificates issued in Bangladesh. According to my information she amount involved for the EU as a whole is some ECU 80‐90 m. The goods have been imported over the last three years, and the importers calculated their prices without taking account of duty, relying in good faith on the certificates issued by the Bengali authorities. The reclaiming of duty will involve large losses for small and medium‐sized undertakings, and will undoubtedly drive some to bankruptcy.

What does the Commission propose to do to prevent this reassessment from being imposed on importers?

It was precisely to prevent this situation that the Council instructed the Commission in 1996 to submit a proposal by the end of 1996 on the creation of a system to reduce the liability of importers. When can this proposal be expected?

Answer

The Commission fully understands the reaction of importers who find themselves liable for the payment of sometimes large amounts of duty several years following the import of goods. However, in accordance with the established precedents of the Court of Justice, the Commission continues to adhere to the principle of the commercial risk borne by importers. The Commission does not consider that the recent judgment in the ‚Seafood’ affair has changed this case‐law.

Consequently, any importer who wants to benefit from preferential tariffs must also bear responsibility for complying with the conditions for enjoying such preferential treatment. He must show the greatest care in this respect. It is the importer who ultimately bears the responsibility for incorrect certificates. A number of remedies could be used in the future in order to limit this risk. These include the ‚compulsory information on origin’, made available to importers since the beginning of the year, and the system of ‚early warnings’ published by the Commission in the Official Journal every time well‐founded suspicions of irregularity come to light in relations with a specific third country.

In regard to the payment of amounts due, the Commission would draw attention to the fact that the Community Customs Code allows customs authorities to grant payment facilities, namely in the form of payment by instalments when immediate recovery of the full amount could cause serious difficulties for the importer.

Concerning the Council Decision of 28 May 1996, on examining the overall nature of the problems this raises the Commission decided to postpone its response in order to be able to cover all these aspects in its communication on the functioning of preferential systems. This communication should be ready for early summer.

***

Question No 84 by Per Gahrton (H−0279/97)

Subject: The EU and the phasing out of nuclear power in Sweden

The Swedish government has recently decided to begin in 1998 with the phasing out of nuclear power in Sweden which was decided upon in a referendum in 1980. In the subsequent debate in Sweden supporters of nuclear energy have expressed 'hopes' that the Commission could in some way prevent or hinder this process. One way in which it might do this would be to intervene against the planned measures aimed at keeping Swedish‐produced electricity within Sweden, e.g. by giving the Swedish National Grid a monopoly of new foreign links (Prof. Ulf Bernitz, Svenska Dagbladet 7 March 1997 – 'The Barsebäck decision will lead to trouble with EU law'). Another way would be to take action against planned tax breaks for the most heavily electricity‐dependent Swedish businesses (Carl B Hamilton, economist, writing in Dagens Nyheter, 22 February 1997: 'The EU can halt the agreement').

Can the Commission make clear that it will not by any direct or indirect means hinder or prevent the plans to phase out nuclear power in Sweden?

Answer

So far, the Swedish Government has not notified any measure it would like to take to hinder electricity exports from Sweden to the European continent. The only existing undersea cable (Baltic Cable) is in private property and it can hardly be seen how the Swedish Grid Company Kraftnätt could get any influence on its operation.

The situation could be different for future new cable interconnections. Any measures to restrict intercommunity trade, like special transborder tariffs or similar solutions, would have to be examined by the Commission whether they are compatible with the EC Treaty, in particular Articles 9, 30 and 34. In case of breach of rules, the Commission would be obliged to take the appropriate measures.

As far as the tax‐exemption of high electricity‐intensive industries is concerned, the Commission is of the opinion that in the absence of an EC energy tax scheme Member States are free to develop their own systems. In this context, tax exemptions are acceptable, as long as they remain within reasonable limits, e.g. those of the new proposal for an EC energy tax.

In general, the Commission does in no way want to influence Member States in their choice of fuels for electricity generation. On the other hand, the Commission attaches very much importance to the free trade of electricity, which is regarded as one of the most decisive factors for the good functioning of the Internal Energy Market.

Summing up, the Commission does not see any reason to object to the Swedish plan for nuclear phaseout, as long as the measures to actually carry out this plan are compatible with EC law.

Question No 85 by Lone Dybkjær (H−0280/97)

Subject: Health and consumer policy

It was argued very strongly in connection with the BSE crisis that health policy needs to be strengthened at Community level. The Commission has accordingly produced a specific proposal for reinforcing Article 129. One of the proposal's aims is that the EU should now have responsibility for harmonizing the health legislation of the Member States. It may seem surprising that a start was not made with consumer protection, which is precisely the area that the BSE crisis shows to be in need of strengthening (Article 129A).

Does the Commission have plans to strengthen Article 129A? If so, what are these specific plans? If not, why not?

Answer

As the Honourable Member has pointed out, the BSE crisis has shown the need to reinforce the protection of consumer health at Community level. This is why the Commission has decided to radically restructure its services on the basis of the principle of the separation of responsibilities, with responsibility for legislation on the one hand, and responsibility for the control and management of scientific committees on the other. The latter task is to be transferred to DG XXIV, responsible for consumer policy and the protection of consumer health.

The amendment to Article 129 of the EC Treaty, as proposed within the Intergovernmental Conference, is a response to this same concern to reinforce Community action in the protection of human health.

The Commission is also following with interest another proposal put forward at the Intergovernmental Conference. This seeks to reinforce Article 129A by incorporating in it a specific provision making it compulsory to take consumer protection into account when deciding and implementing other Community policies and measures.

Question No 86 by Ana Palacio Vallelersundi (H−0281/97)

Subject: Distortion of competition for language schools

The French Ministry of Youth and Sport provides financial aid for young French people residing in France to take part in holiday courses outside France, on condition that the establishment organizing the course is registered in France (Decree 90‐64 of 29 January 1960).

This Ministry has refused to register a private English language school registered in the United Kingdom and located in Bournemouth on the pretext that it cannot apply French rules to a foreign organization. This decision obviously means that this school will not have any students from France.

What action does the Commission intend to take in respect of this national protectionist measure which violates Community legislation regarding a single market?

Answer

The Commission thanks the honourable Member for drawing this matter to its attention. It will examine the matter carefully, and take the appropriate action if any Community rules have been violated.

Question No 87 by Christine Oddy (H−0284/97)

Subject: Follow‐up to the World Summit for Social Development

What steps has the Commission taken as a result of the 1995 agreement signed by 117 countries after the World Summit for Social Development?

Will the Commission support the European statement on poverty, which calls for an international approach to the measurement and explanation of poverty and was signed by European social scientists on 11 March 1997?

Answer

In February the Commission adopted a Communication to the Council and Parliament setting down how it proposes to put into effect the agreements made at the Copenhagen Social Summit and affirming its resolve to focus its action on five issues which deserve particular attention: incorporating social aspects into the international framework; promoting soil and human development in bilateral agreements; stepping up the fight against poverty through development actions and continuing efforts to combat marginalization with the Community; to keep employment as a top priority for economic and social policy; to ensure respect and protection for immigrants as well as fighting against racism and xenophobia.

The choice of these five themes in no way reduces the need for the Community to continue its efforts to table common positions on the range of issues arising from the Summit to be followed up in the UN. The Community also subscribes to the notion that civil society should play a major role in economic and social development and intends to take practical initiatives to achieve this. Similarly, in a bid to promote the principles of the integrated approach advanced in Copenhagen and ensure that the Community fulfils its undertakings, the Commission also proposes that expert meetings be held to examine the extent to which the undertakings made at the Summit are coming to fruition.

It is against this background that the Commission welcomes the recent (17/3/97) statement issued by the European social scientists calling for a more international approach to the measurement and explanation of poverty. The Commission supports the idea of continued dialogue and research aimed at promoting a more converging view and understanding of poverty and how to measure it. Such a dialogue and research would facilitate and enhance the process of exchange of both ‚best practice’ and ‚best policy’ between countries in the field of combatting poverty.

***

Question No 88 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0285/97)

Subject: Declassification and change of use of woodland in Greece

The Greek Government is reported to be pressing ahead with the declassification of 5 200 000 hectares of woodland and the allocation of public land to private individuals so as to allow it to be re‐used for building. In the Attica Basin alone 2 200 hectares are due to be used for construction, thus significantly exacerbating the serious environmental pollution in the Athens region, despite the fact that a previous law with the same content was ruled unconstitutional.

Will the Commission say whether actions of this kind are contrary to Community legislation, in particular environmental protection legislation, and whether it intends to take practical initiatives to support the establishment of a forestry registry and a more general land utilization plan for Greece, the updating of forestry legislation and the establishment of a forestry charter?

Answer

The situation envisaged by the Honourable Member, based on press reports, does not lie within the Community's field of competence and is not an infringement of the Community's environmental protection legislation.

Land use and town and country planning are in principle the responsibility of the Member States. On 4 December 1996 the Commission adopted a proposal for a directive concerning the evaluation of the impact of certain plans and programmes on the environment. When this directive is adopted, the actions mentioned by the Honourable Member could lie within its field of application and would then be the subject of an impact evaluation.

However, if the declassification were to concern special protection areas as designated in the framework of Directive 79/409/EEC (protection of wild birds) or sites included on the national list of biotopes to be protected under Directive 93/42/EEC (habitats), the Commission could then approach the responsible Greek authorities, if the declassification affected their protected status.

If, following the adoption of a formal act on this subject, deforestation or another project included in the annexes to Directive 85/337/EEC (evaluation of environmental impact) was decided, this would then have to be the subject of an environmental impact study by the terms of the aforementioned directive.

If the Honourable Member is able to gather further information on this subject which indicates infractions of Community environmental law, the Commission will most certainly examine it and, if applicable, contact the responsible Greek authorities in order to ensure that Community environmental legislation is strictly respected.

As to the initiatives proposed by the Honourable Member, work has just begun on establishing a registry. This is being co‐financed by the Structural Funds under the ‚Environment’ operational programme of the Community Support Framework II, while the updating of forestry legislation and the establishment of a forestry charter are the exclusive responsibility of the individual Member State.

***

Question No 89 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0286/97)

Subject: Fatal accidents in Perama shipyards and health and safety rules at the workplace

There has been an increase in the number of industrial accidents in the shipbuilding sector in Greece where a number of workers have been killed and injured, owing to the absence of even the most basic safety precautions. The situation is particularly dramatic in the Eleusis shipyards, especially in the ship repair zone. Faced with the threat of unemployment – the rate is over 35 % in the Perama region in general, and over 70 % in the shipyards in particular‐ 60 % of workers are forced to work without insurance and without being declared, on low wages and under unacceptable health and safety conditions imposed on them by their employers for the sake of 'competitiveness'.

Will the Commission say what measures it intends to take to ensure that national and Community health and safety legislation in the workplace is implemented so as to avoid a recurrence of the tragic events that have taken place at the Perama ship repair zone, that collective bargaining agreements are respected and that the shipbuilding industry is strengthened with a view to creating new jobs and maintaining the social fabric of one of the most important industrial regions in Greece?

Answer

The Commission would like to reassure the Honourable Member that Greece has adopted and communicated to the Commission all the national implementing measures of the health and safety at the workplace directives applicable to the shipbuilding sector.

In accordance with Article 4, paragraph 2 of Directive 89/392/EEC(3) , it is the responsibility of the Greek work inspectorate to ensure that employers respect the applicable Greek regulations. At the same time, the Commission checks that the Member States correctly and effectively transpose secondary legislation measures.

Although the analysis of Greece's transposition of the aforementioned directive is not yet complete, it does seem that it was correctly transposed. Nevertheless, if problems in applying national transposition measures were shown to exist, the Commission could decide to instigate infringement procedures pursuant to Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

The Commission plans to publish a Green Paper on undeclared work in 1997. This will look at possible ways of changing the present system of taxation, control and related structures in order to incorporate in the official labour market the many workers who are at present marginalized by working on the black market. Its aim is to relaunch the debate between the social partners and public authorities.

In regard to social cover, it should be pointed out that, under present Community law, the organization and operation of social security systems and the coverage of workers by these systems are a matter solely for the individual Member States.

The Commission also stresses that its policy and action in this sector are essentially designed to support the efforts of industrialists to make the industry more viable and internationally competitive. Only a competitive industry can guarantee this sector and its workforce their rightful place.

Question No 90 by Frederik Willockx (H−0288/97)

Subject: Statement made on 20 March 1997 by the President of the Commission, Mr Santer, before the Belgian Parliament's Consultative Committee on European Affairs

At the meeting referred to above, the President of the Commission stated that the Belgian franc would perhaps fare less well without the assistance of the banks in Luxembourg(4) . He said as much during his reply in which he discussed issues concerning the lack of fiscal rules and problems which investigations into tax fraud encountered because of bank secrecy. Is such a statement in conformity with the duties of the President of the European Commission, who is, after all, held to act independently of the interests of the Member States and whose job it is to defend the Community interest and respect for the Treaties? Is this statement in conformity with the efforts being made in the High‐Level Working Party on Taxation in which the Member States, under the chairmanship of the Commission, are trying to devise a strategy and procedure which should result in fiscal coordination at European level?

Answer

The President answered many questions in the debate which followed his address to the Belgian Parliament's Consultative Committee on European Affairs and which was devoted to the IGC and major dates on the European calendar.

At the end of this debate, one Member felt obliged to put a question to the President which directly called into question the Luxembourg financial centre and its fiscal policy. The President replied in his capacity as the man who had been this Member State's prime minister for 11 years and finance minister for 15 years, the question clearly not being directed at the Commission President but at the individual who was previously responsible for Luxembourg's financial policy.

The President clearly did not in any way call into question the work of the High‐Level Working Party on Taxation, chaired by Mr Monti. It should be pointed out that this committee was set up following a proposal by the Commission of which Mr Santer is President. Also, in accordance with the oath taken before the Court of Justice of the European Communities, the President exercises his function in total independence, in the Community's general interest.

Question No 91 by Anne Mcintosh (H−1034/96)

Subject: Compensation for UK road hauliers after French lorry blockade

Bearing in mind that the recent French lorry blockade has cost the UK and many countries' road haulage industry millions of pounds, both in loss of earnings and of perishable goods, does the Commission intend to take action to secure the appropriate compensation from the French authorities for those road hauliers affected by the blockade? If so, what measures are being planned?

Does the Commission not agree that the failure of the French authorities to keep haulage traffic moving was negligent, being both an infringement on the freedom of movement provisions in the Treaty on European Union, and of the EU directive on drivers' hours?

Answer

As the House will know from previous statements, the Commission was very concerned by the hindrances to the free movement of persons and goods during the recent French lorry blockade and has therefore intervened strongly with the French Government during and since the dispute.

On the specific question of compensation for losses suffered as a result of the blockades, the Commission has no legal authority to intervene since there is no Community law which covers the right to compensation for individuals or companies affected by strike action. However, the House will be familiar with the undertakings given publicly by the French authorities that they are in the process of compensating foreign companies on the basis of French law, and the House will also know that the government established arrangements for local prefectures to provide claim forms for compensation.

The Commission has no effective legal right or ability to intervene in a national dispute between employers and trade unions unless a Member State can be shown to be negligent in its duty to comply with its obligations under the Treaty, one of which is the duty to uphold the legal freedom of movement of goods and persons.

As the House will know, in the case of last year's strike and disruption the French Government made consistent efforts to maintain or restore freedom of movement by seeking to resolve the dispute.EN (Lennel) – 3‐091A
